  <!-- Layout -->
  
  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">God and Poetry</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
  <div class="content brown-course-detail">
		
  	<h3>JUDS 0820 S01 <span class="crn">[<abbr title="Course Reference Number">CRN</abbr>: 15140]</span></h3>


  	In this seminar we will read selections from the biblical books of Psalms and Job and contemporary Jewish and Christian poetry, exploring how the language of poetry can serve as a means to convey the nature of relations between humanity and the divine.   We will also discuss the underlying universal human psychological experiences reflected in the poems and how religiosity provides a framework for people to deal with those experiences.  In our discussion of the contemporary Jewish and Christian poems, we will seek to understand the attempt of the poet to write about religious experience in a secular age. WRIT
		
		

		<dl>
		
		
		
		
		
		



					<dt>Term</dt>
			<dd>Fall 2017</dd>
		
		
		
		


		

	
					<dt>Meetings</dt>
									<dd>
						<span class="room"> </span>
						<span class="times">10:00 - 10:50 Mon, Wed, Fri - from Sep 6, 2017 to Dec 21, 2017</span>
						</dd>
							
		
		
		
		


		</dl>
  </div>

 </div>
                     </div>
        <!-- end .second-column -->

  <div id="content">

                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Holocaust Literature</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
  <div class="content brown-course-detail">
		
  	<h3>JUDS 1820 S01 <span class="crn">[<abbr title="Course Reference Number">CRN</abbr>: 15141]</span></h3>


  	Readings in works of prose and poetry by victims and survivors of the Holocaust that portray experiences in ghettos, in concentration camps, and in hiding. Additional readings in works of the post-war era by survivors and their offspring. Discussion of the moral, psychological, religious, and cultural dimensions of the Holocaust and its ongoing impact on humanity. WRIT
		
		

		<dl>
		
		
		
		
		
		



					<dt>Term</dt>
			<dd>Fall 2017</dd>
		
					<dt>Credit Hours</dt>
			<dd>1</dd>
		
					<dt>Maximum Enrollment</dt>
			<dd>999</dd>
		
					<dt>Course Attributes</dt>
						<dd>Writing - Designated Courses</dd>
			
		


												
	<dt>Primary Instructor</dt>

		<dd>
		<div class="vcard">
			<div>
						<a class="url fn" href="http://directory.brown.edu/search?search_string=David+C+Jacobson">David C Jacobson</a>
			</div>
			
			
		</div>
	</dd>
				
			

	
					<dt>Meetings</dt>
									<dd>
						<span class="room"> </span>
						<span class="times">11:00 - 11:50 Mon, Wed, Fri - from Sep 6, 2017 to Dec 21, 2017</span>
						</dd>
							
		
		
		
					<dt>Exam Group Code</dt>
			<dd>16</dd>
		


		</dl>
  </div>

 </div>
                     </div>

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Israel's Wars</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
  <div class="content brown-course-detail">
		
  	<h3>JUDS 0050H S01 <span class="crn">[<abbr title="Course Reference Number">CRN</abbr>: 15142]</span></h3>


  	Israel's history has unfolded under the shadow of its prolonged conflict with the Palestinians and its Arab neighbors. This first year seminar will survey the military aspect of this conflict. The major aim of the course is to present an historical survey of the Israeli-Arab wars and Jewish-Palestinian encounters in the 20th century. This will provide some of the necessary background for understanding the present phase of the Arab-Israeli conflict in the Middle East, and help in comprehending the roots and causes of contemporary controversies between Israel and the Palestinians and/or its Arab neighboring states. Enrollment limited to 20 first year students. FYS
		
		

		<dl>
		
		
		
		
		
		



					<dt>Term</dt>
			<dd>Fall 2017</dd>
		
					<dt>Credit Hours</dt>
			<dd>1</dd>
		
					<dt>Maximum Enrollment</dt>
			<dd>20</dd>
		
					<dt>Course Attributes</dt>
						<dd>First Year Seminar</dd>
			
		


												
	<dt>Primary Instructor</dt>

		<dd>
		<div class="vcard">
			<div>
						<a class="url fn" href="http://directory.brown.edu/search?search_string=Rachel+Rojanski">Rachel Rojanski</a>
			</div>
			
			
		</div>
	</dd>
				
			

	
					<dt>Meetings</dt>
									<dd>
						<span class="room"> </span>
						<span class="times">14:30 - 15:50 Tue, Thu - from Sep 6, 2017 to Dec 21, 2017</span>
						</dd>
							
		
		
		
					<dt>Exam Group Code</dt>
			<dd>03</dd>
		


		</dl>
  </div>

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Introduction to Yiddish Culture and Language</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
  <div class="content brown-course-detail">
		
  	<h3>JUDS 1713 S01 <span class="crn">[<abbr title="Course Reference Number">CRN</abbr>: 15143]</span></h3>


  	Yiddish was the language spoken by most Jews in Eastern Europe and the countries to which they emigrated (including the U.S., England, South Africa, South American countries, and Israel) from the nineteenth century until after the Holocaust. It was the basis for a transnational Jewish culture and literature, and it played a central role in modern Jewish political life. We will explore the history of Yiddish culture and the development of the Yiddish press, literature, and cinema. The connection between Yiddish and modern Jewish politics will also be discussed. Students in this course will also have the opportunity to develop a basic knowledge of the Yiddish language. DPLL
		
		

		<dl>
		
		
		
		
		
		



					<dt>Term</dt>
			<dd>Fall 2017</dd>
		
					<dt>Credit Hours</dt>
			<dd>1</dd>
		
					<dt>Maximum Enrollment</dt>
			<dd>10</dd>
		
					<dt>Course Attributes</dt>
						<dd>Diverse Perspectives in LL</dd>
			
		


												
	<dt>Primary Instructor</dt>

		<dd>
		<div class="vcard">
			<div>
						<a class="url fn" href="http://directory.brown.edu/search?search_string=Rachel+Rojanski">Rachel Rojanski</a>
			</div>
			
			
		</div>
	</dd>
				
			

	
					<dt>Meetings</dt>
									<dd>
						<span class="room"> </span>
						<span class="times">16:00 - 18:30 Thu - from Sep 6, 2017 to Dec 21, 2017</span>
						</dd>
							
		
		
		
					<dt>Exam Group Code</dt>
			<dd>04</dd>
		


		</dl>
  </div>

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Problems in Israelite History</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
  <div class="content brown-course-detail">
		
  	<h3>JUDS 1635 S01 <span class="crn">[<abbr title="Course Reference Number">CRN</abbr>: 15153]</span></h3>


  	Topics of recent and current debate among specialists in the field of Israelite history.  Problems include (1) the historicity of the patriarchs and matriarchs; (2) the historical evidence relevant to the question of an exodus; (3) the nature of Israel's settlement in Canaan; (4) the 10th century, era of empire or literary fiction? (5) the land of Judah after the Babylonian conquest. Enrollment limited to 20.
		
		

		<dl>
		
		
		
		
		
		



					<dt>Term</dt>
			<dd>Fall 2017</dd>
		
					<dt>Credit Hours</dt>
			<dd>1</dd>
		
					<dt>Maximum Enrollment</dt>
			<dd>10</dd>
		
		


												
	<dt>Primary Instructor</dt>

		<dd>
		<div class="vcard">
			<div>
						<a class="url fn" href="http://directory.brown.edu/search?search_string=Saul+Olyan">Saul Olyan</a>
			</div>
			
			
		</div>
	</dd>
				
			

	
					<dt>Meetings</dt>
									<dd>
						<span class="room"> </span>
						<span class="times">15:00 - 17:30 Wed - from Sep 6, 2017 to Dec 21, 2017</span>
						</dd>
							
		
		
		
					<dt>Exam Group Code</dt>
			<dd>17</dd>
		


		</dl>
  </div>

 </div>
                     </div>
        <!-- end .second-column -->

  <!-- Layout -->
  
  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Race, Religion, and the Secular</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
  <div class="content brown-course-detail">
		
  	<h3>JUDS 0603 S01 <span class="crn">[<abbr title="Course Reference Number">CRN</abbr>: 16545]</span></h3>


  	The most "secular" presidential election in American history saw the language of Christian America apparently yield to a rhetoric of racism, misogyny, and white identity. But racialization and secularization are very much intertwined. In an effort to understand how “whiteness” is tied to the history of Christianity and secular, liberal democracies, this class will trace the figure of the "Jew" in the Christian imagination, and examine the racial and religious othering of Judaism as an entry point for reflecting upon contemporary American social and political struggles surrounding religious and racial identities. WRIT DPLL
		
		

		<dl>
		
		
		
		
		
		



					<dt>Term</dt>
			<dd>Fall 2017</dd>
		
					<dt>Credit Hours</dt>
			<dd>1</dd>
		
					<dt>Maximum Enrollment</dt>
			<dd>30</dd>
		
					<dt>Course Attributes</dt>
						<dd></dd>
			
		


												
	<dt>Primary Instructor</dt>

		<dd>
		<div class="vcard">
			<div>
						<a class="url fn" href="http://directory.brown.edu/search?search_string=Paul+E+Nahme">Paul E Nahme</a>
			</div>
			
			
		</div>
	</dd>
				
			

	
					<dt>Meetings</dt>
									<dd>
						<span class="room"> </span>
						<span class="times">13:00 - 14:20 Tue, Thu - from Sep 6, 2017 to Dec 21, 2017</span>
						</dd>
							
		
		
		
					<dt>Exam Group Code</dt>
			<dd>10</dd>
		


		</dl>
  </div>

 </div>
                     </div>
        <!-- end .second-column -->

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">The Ten Commandments</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
  <div class="content brown-course-detail">
		
  	<h3>JUDS 0686 S01 <span class="crn">[<abbr title="Course Reference Number">CRN</abbr>: 17123]</span></h3>


  	A history of the Ten Commandments from the Bible to today.  How have the commandments been understood by Jews and Christians throughout time?  What symbolic importance have they had?  WRIT
		
		

		<dl>
		
		
		
		
		
		



					<dt>Term</dt>
			<dd>Fall 2017</dd>
		
					<dt>Credit Hours</dt>
			<dd>1</dd>
		
					<dt>Maximum Enrollment</dt>
			<dd>40</dd>
		
					<dt>Course Attributes</dt>
						<dd>Writing - Designated Courses</dd>
			
		


												
	<dt>Primary Instructor</dt>

		<dd>

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Courses for Fall 2017</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> <div class="content">



		<div class="course-section" id="JUDS">
			
				<ul class="course-list">
										<li id="JUDS0050H">
					<h4>Israel's Wars</h4>
					<div class="course-overview">Israel's history has unfolded under the shadow of its prolonged conflict with the Palestinians and its Arab neighbors. This first year seminar will survey the military aspect of this conflict. The major aim of the course is to present an historical survey of the Israeli-Arab wars and Jewish-Palestinian encounters in the 20th century. This will provide some of the necessary background for understanding the present phase of the Arab-Israeli conflict in the Middle East, and help in comprehending the roots and causes of contemporary controversies between Israel and the Palestinians and/or its Arab neighboring states. Enrollment limited to 20 first year students. FYS </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=15142">JUDS 0050H S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																<dt>Primary Instructor</dt><dd>Rojanski</dd>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS0062">
					<h4>Intro to the New Testament (RELS 0022)</h4>
					<div class="course-overview"> </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=17143">JUDS 0062 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS0602">
					<h4>Gender in Early Jewish and Christian Texts (RELS 0195)</h4>
					<div class="course-overview"> </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=17144">JUDS 0602 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS0603">
					<h4>Race, Religion, and the Secular</h4>
					<div class="course-overview">The most "secular" presidential election in American history saw the language of Christian America apparently yield to a rhetoric of racism, misogyny, and white identity. But racialization and secularization are very much intertwined. In an effort to understand how “whiteness” is tied to the history of Christianity and secular, liberal democracies, this class will trace the figure of the "Jew" in the Christian imagination, and examine the racial and religious othering of Judaism as an entry point for reflecting upon contemporary American social and political struggles surrounding religious and racial identities. WRIT DPLL </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=16545">JUDS 0603 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																<dt>Primary Instructor</dt><dd>Nahme</dd>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS0685">
					<h4>Judaism, Christianity, and Islam (RELS 0088)</h4>
					<div class="course-overview"> </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=16907">JUDS 0685 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS0686">
					<h4>The Ten Commandments</h4>
					<div class="course-overview">A history of the Ten Commandments from the Bible to today.  How have the commandments been understood by Jews and Christians throughout time?  What symbolic importance have they had?  WRIT </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=17123">JUDS 0686 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																<dt>Primary Instructor</dt><dd>Satlow</dd>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS1635">
					<h4>Problems in Israelite History</h4>
					<div class="course-overview">Topics of recent and current debate among specialists in the field of Israelite history.  Problems include (1) the historicity of the patriarchs and matriarchs; (2) the historical evidence relevant to the question of an exodus; (3) the nature of Israel's settlement in Canaan; (4) the 10th century, era of empire or literary fiction? (5) the land of Judah after the Babylonian conquest. Enrollment limited to 20. </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=15153">JUDS 1635 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																<dt>Primary Instructor</dt><dd>Olyan</dd>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS1713">
					<h4>Introduction to Yiddish Culture and Language</h4>
					<div class="course-overview">Yiddish was the language spoken by most Jews in Eastern Europe and the countries to which they emigrated (including the U.S., England, South Africa, South American countries, and Israel) from the nineteenth century until after the Holocaust. It was the basis for a transnational Jewish culture and literature, and it played a central role in modern Jewish political life. We will explore the history of Yiddish culture and the development of the Yiddish press, literature, and cinema. The connection between Yiddish and modern Jewish politics will also be discussed. Students in this course will also have the opportunity to develop a basic knowledge of the Yiddish language. DPLL </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=15143">JUDS 1713 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																<dt>Primary Instructor</dt><dd>Rojanski</dd>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS1820">
					<h4>Holocaust Literature</h4>
					<div class="course-overview">Readings in works of prose and poetry by victims and survivors of the Holocaust that portray experiences in ghettos, in concentration camps, and in hiding. Additional readings in works of the post-war era by survivors and their offspring. Discussion of the moral, psychological, religious, and cultural dimensions of the Holocaust and its ongoing impact on humanity. WRIT </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=15141">JUDS 1820 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>
																<dt>Primary Instructor</dt><dd>Jacobson</dd>
																							</dl>
						</div>
						
																					
																								</div>
						
				</li>
										<li id="JUDS1970">
					<h4>Individual Study Projects</h4>
					<div class="course-overview">Section numbers vary by instructor. Please see Banner for the correct  course reference number (CRN) to use when registering for this course. </div>
		
					<div class="sections">
								
												
												<div class="section first-section">
												
							<h5><a href="/academics/judaic-studies/COURSES/detail?term=201710&crn=13462">JUDS 1970 S01</a></h5>
														<dl>

<script type="text/javascript">
	// @todo: make show/hide link dynamically generated
	$(document).ready(function(){
	
		$(".course-list .additional-sections").hide();

		// add additional sections toggle link
		$(".course-list .additional-sections").each(function(i,el){
			var numSections = $(".section", $(this)).length;
			numSections = (numSections == 1) ? numSections + " additional section" : numSections + " additional sections";
			var anchor = $(this).parents("li").attr('id');
			$(this).after('<span class="section-count">[<a href="#' + anchor + '">Show ' + numSections + '</a>]</span>');
		});

		// now add the show/hide event listeners		
		$(".section-count").each(function(i,el){
			$("a", this).bind("click keypress", function(){
				$(".additional-sections", $(this).parents("li")).slideToggle("fast");
				window.location.hash = $(this).attr("href");
				if($(this).html().match(/Show/)) {
					$(this).html($(this).html().replace("Show", "Hide"));
				} else if($(this).html().match(/Hide/)) {
					$(this).html($(this).html().replace("Hide", "Show"));
				}
				this.text(($('#toggle').text() == 'Show more') ? 'Show less' : 'Show more');
				return false;
			});
		});
		
		if (window.location.hash) {
			var hash = window.location.hash;
			if(hash.match(/#[0-9]{4}/)) {
				$(hash +  " .additional-sections").slideDown("fast");
				$(hash +  " a[href='"+hash+"']").html($(hash +  " a[href='"+hash+"']").html().replace("Show", "Hide"));
			}
		}

	});
</script>

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Graduate Program</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">

<article id="node-13" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p>Graduate study in Hebrew Bible, Ancient Judaism and Jewish thought is housed int he Department of Religious Studies. &nbsp;Visit <a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/religious-studies/graduate-study">http://www.brown.edu/academics/religious-studies/graduate-study</a>&nbsp;to learn more.&nbsp;</p>

                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Program News:  Brown Judaic Studies welcomes Visiting Scholar, Thomas C. Römer</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 

<article class="content news_story clear-block main-article">

<div class="p details">
    <span class="date">January 16, 2015</span>
</div>






<div style="width: 300px; " class="inline-right photo-wrapper main-image">
	  <a href="/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/images/news/2011/thomas-romer-cover-small_0.jpg" class="thickbox"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/imagecache/ursa_feature_image/images/news/2011/thomas-romer-cover-small_0.jpg" alt="" title="" width="300" height="300" class="imagecache imagecache-ursa_feature_image"/></a>
  				<div class="caption">
			<strong class="image-title">Prof. Thomas Römer</strong>								</div>
		  </div>





	<div class="body">
		<p>The Brown Judaic Studies Visiting Scholar in 2015 is <strong>Thomas C. Römer</strong>, Professor at the Collège de France (Paris) and the Université de Lausanne. Römer is the author of many influential books and articles on the Hebrew Bible and the world out of which it emerged. He has been a visiting professor in Montpellier, Neuchâtel, Zürich, Pretoria and Managua, and Sackler Scholar at the University of Tel Aviv, among other honors. He will present a public lecture on Tuesday, Feb. 10 at 5:30 PM, <span>at&nbsp;</span><span>Brown RISD Hillel at 80 Brown Street, Meeting Room,&nbsp;</span>entitled “Yhwh, a God of the Wilderness: A Biblical and Extrabiblical Investigation.”</p>
	</div>

	

	<fieldset class="fieldgroup group-related-files"><legend>Related Files</legend>
		<div class="field field-type-filefield field-field-related-files">

			</div>
		</div>
	</fieldset>




</article>
 </div>

  <div id="content">

                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Program News:  Spring 2015 Judaic Studies Faculty Seminars </h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 

<article class="content news_story clear-block main-article">

<div class="p details">
    <span class="date">March 10, 2015</span>
</div>









	<div class="body">
		<p><strong>Judaic Studies Faculty Seminar Series - Spring 2015<br /></strong><strong>Location:</strong><span>&nbsp; 163 George Street, Seminar Room 206<br /></span><strong>Time:</strong><span>&nbsp; Beginning at noon<br /></span><strong>Day:</strong><span> Thursdays<br /></span><em>Lunch provided with RSVP</em></p>
<p><span>Upcoming Judaic Studies&nbsp;faculty&nbsp;seminars&nbsp;scheduled for spring 2014 are open to all Judaic Studies faculty, affiliates, and invited friends. &nbsp;For more information, please contact<span>&nbsp;</span><a href="mailto:susan_rottenberg@brown.edu" rel="nofollow"><span>susan_rottenberg@brown.edu</span></a>.</span></p>
<p align="center"><strong><span style="text-decoration:underline">SPRING 2015</span></strong></p>
<p><strong>February 5<sup>th<br /></sup></strong><span>Thomas Römer (Collège de France/Université de Lausanne)<br /></span><span>"The Invention of the 'Sojourn in the Wilderness' in the Book of Numbers"</span></p>
<p><strong>March 19<sup>th<br /></sup></strong><span>Mary Gluck"<br /></span><span>"Jewish Humor and Self-Parody in Fin-De-Siecle Budapest"</span></p>
<p><strong>April 16<sup>th</sup> <sup>&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;<br /></sup></strong><span>Adam Teller<br /></span><span>“Print, Power, and Prestige: &nbsp;</span><span>The Polish-Lithuanian Rabbinate and the Book in the Sixteenth and Seventeenth Centuries”</span></p>
	</div>

	




</article>
 </div>
                     </div>

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Program News:  UNIV1001 Spends Spring Break Immersed in the Israeli-Palestinian Conflict</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 

<article class="content news_story clear-block main-article">

<div class="p details">
    <span class="date">April 20, 2015</span>
</div>






<div style="width: 510px; " class="inline-right photo-wrapper main-image">
	  <a href="/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/images/news/2011/2014_15_UNIV1001_Pic1.jpg" class="thickbox"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/imagecache/ursa_feature_image/images/news/2011/2014_15_UNIV1001_Pic1.jpg" alt="" title="" width="510" height="383" class="imagecache imagecache-ursa_feature_image"/></a>
  				<div class="caption">
						<span class="image-description"><p><span>Students, Professor David Jacobson, and Tour Guide Ilan Bloch on a hilltop overlooking Israel's Negev region.</span></p>
</span>					</div>
		  </div>





	<div class="body">
		<p><span>With the generous support of a Global Experiential Learning and Teaching (GELT) Grant from the Office of Global Engagement and additional funds provided by the Program in Judaic Studies, the twelve Brown juniors and seniors in my seminar, “The Israeli-Palestinian Conflict: Contested Narratives,” spent seven days beginning shortly before Spring Break touring the land both peoples claim to be their own and hearing a wide range of Palestinian and Israeli voices expressing their understanding of the history of the conflict and the hopes and fears they experience after so many decades of fighting.</span></p>
<p>During the weeks leading up to the trip, the seminar was devoted to exploring the narratives of the conflict, the conflicting stories that each side tells each other and the world about how the conflict began, why it has persisted, and whether there is any viable way to resolve it.&nbsp; The sources of these narratives included historical documents, works of fiction, and feature films.&nbsp;&nbsp; Well-equipped with knowledge of the conflict, the students greatly deepened their understanding during the week of the trip, and the new insights they discovered have informed their continued study of the conflict upon their return to campus.</p>
<p>Highlights of the trip included a bus tour around Jerusalem led by the NGO City of Peoples, dedicated to educating the public about the impact of the Palestinian-Israeli conflict on the city that each people claims as its capital; a walking tour of the religious sites in the Old City of Jerusalem holy to Jews, Christians, and Muslims; a visit to Mount Herzl in Jerusalem to view the graves of Israeli soldiers who died in battle and of prominent Israeli leaders, as well as the tomb of Theodor Herzl, seen by Israelis as the founding father of Zionism; visits to the tomb of Palestinian leader Yasser Arafat and a museum dedicated to Palestinian poet Mahmoud Darwish in Ramallah on the West Bank; a visit to Israel’s Yad Vashem Holocaust Museum; a tour of Lifta, a village abandoned by Palestinians in the 1948 Arab-Israeli War; a visit to Arab Bedouin towns in Israel’s Negev region, where students learned about ongoing land disputes between the Israeli government and local residents; &nbsp;a tour of parts of the ancient port city of Jaffa and the modern Israeli city of Tel Aviv; and dinner at the home of a Palestinian family. &nbsp;At lunch in Ramallah students had the opportunity to meet and speak with Sami Jarbawi ’12, a Palestinian who worked with me on the UTRA in which the course was designed.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Conversations with Israeli and Palestinian speakers provided a fascinating exposure to conflicting world views between these enemy peoples and within each people.&nbsp; Two Israeli political figures analyzed the Israeli parliamentary election which took place right before we arrived: Dani Dayan, a right-wing supporter of the re-election of Prime Minister Binyamin Netanyahu, and Mosi Raz, who ran on the ticket of the left-wing Meretz Party. &nbsp;Bob Lang, a religious West-Bank Jewish settler in Efrat, shared with us the settler narrative which supports the right of Jews to settle in the area known as the West Bank, occupied by Israel in 1967, while Eliaz Cohen, a religious West-Bank Jewish settler in Kfar Etzion, told of his vision of how Israeli and Palestinian national aspirations could be achieved in the West Bank without anyone having to be uprooted. &nbsp;Riman Barakat of the think tank Israel Palestine Creative Regional Initiatives (IPCRI) in Jerusalem also told of a similar plan her organization is developing.&nbsp; Reverend Alex</p>
<p>Awad of East Jerusalem told about the unique experiences of the Christian Palestinian minority, while Islamic Scholar Dr. Mustafa Abu Sway of Al Quds University in Jerusalem spoke passionately about the need to end Israel’s occupation of the West Bank, but he also made clear that he believed national identities were human constructs and therefore long-standing arguments about the legitimacy of Israeli and Palestinian rights to the land were beside the point.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Students learned of the national tragedies that continue to haunt both peoples, the Holocaust for Israelis and the Nakba defeat of 1948 for the Palestinians.&nbsp; Holocaust survivor Rina Quint told the amazing story of her survival as a child in a concentration camp and declared that a central message of the Holocaust is that there must be a State of Israel that can serve as a refuge for persecuted Jews. &nbsp;Amal Jadou of the Palestinian Authority Foreign Ministry told of her moving experience going with her father to see the home in West Jerusalem that he left during the 1948 Arab-Israeli War. Vivian Rabia narrated the story of how a house in the Israeli town of Ramle abandoned by a Palestinian family in the 1948 War was turned into a preschool to serve Palestinian children.</p>
<p>Students were particularly moved by their meetings with Palestinians and Israelis who are trying to bridge the social, cultural, and political gaps between the two peoples.&nbsp; Israeli Rami Elhanan told of the Parents Circle Family Forum that brings together Israelis and Palestinians who have lost loved ones in the conflict.&nbsp; Nomika Zion, a resident of the Israeli town of Sderot on the border of the Hamas-controlled Gaza Strip, which has suffered greatly in Palestinian rocket attacks during the past several years, informed the group of an Israeli organization called Other Voice which makes regular contact by telephone with residents of the Gaza Strip.&nbsp; Vivian Rabia of Ramle told of the challenges she faces directing a Palestinian-Israeli summer day camp outside of Jerusalem.&nbsp; Rabbi Daniel Roth of the Pardes Center for Judaism and Conflict Resolution told of his efforts to get Jews to rethink their views of their Arab enemies.</p>
<p>The trip was organized by MEJDI Tours, which specializes in dual-narrative trips to Israel/Palestine.&nbsp; The tour was led by Palestinian guide Jalal Ghazi Awad and Israeli guide Ilan Bloch, who shared their extensive expertise and at times demonstrated the contested nature of Palestinian and Israeli narratives by arguing openly about how to evaluate the ways each people has acted in the past.</p>
<p>It is clear from all that we saw and heard that for the most part neither Israelis nor Palestinians are currently optimistic about any breakthrough toward peace in the immediate future.&nbsp; Perhaps the starkest assessment of the situation was expressed by Dr. Mustafa Abu Sway, who declared in reference to the possibility of a resolution of the conflict: “I don’t see the light at the end of the tunnel. In fact, I don’t even see the tunnel.”&nbsp;&nbsp; At the same time, &nbsp;we learned, the pessimism that prevails among both Palestinians and Israelis is constantly challenged by those who attempt to bring together people from both sides of the divide and those who dream of alternative solutions.</p>
<p>Students enthusiastically appreciated the rare opportunity that they had to &nbsp;participate in this informative study tour.&nbsp; As Jason Ginsberg ’16 observed: “Reading a textbook is one thing, but this program truly offered me the opportunity to live that textbook—to see the issues in the land as a class in real time.&nbsp; It is a rare gift to be able to engage with material in this way.”&nbsp;&nbsp; Students came back from the trip with new insights, and in some cases the trip radically changed their views of the conflict. “To say that the course and the study tour transformed the way I think about the [Israeli-Palestinian] conflict would be an understatement,” declared Hannah Acheson Field ’15. Joao Nascimento ‘15 saw the study tour as “an opportunity to unlearn my previously entrenched ideas about the conflict between Israelis and Palestinians as much as it was a chance to better understand and see for myself the myriad narratives that animate their dispute.”&nbsp; Reem Rayef ’15 spoke of the impact meeting the people of the Israeli-Palestinian conflict had on her: “This trip complicated everything I knew—or thought I knew—about the conflict, by providing us with the invaluable opportunity to make our course readings come alive in their natural context.”</p>
<p>Submitted by Professor David Jacobson</p>
	</div>

	




</article>
 </div>
                     </div>
        <!-- end .second-column -->

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Program News: Undergraduate Essay Prize in Judaic Studies</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 

<article class="content news_story clear-block main-article">

<div class="p details">
    <span class="date">October 5, 2017</span>
</div>






<div style="width: 348px; " class="inline-right photo-wrapper main-image">
	  <a href="/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/images/news/2011/2018_Michaelson essay prize_flyer_50.jpg" class="thickbox"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/imagecache/ursa_feature_image/images/news/2011/2018_Michaelson%20essay%20prize_flyer_50.jpg" alt="" title="" width="348" height="450" class="imagecache imagecache-ursa_feature_image"/></a>
  		  </div>





	<div class="body">
		<p><span>The Program in Judaic Studies is now accepting applications for the Undergraduate Essay Prize in Judaic Studies. &nbsp;The $500 award will go to the best academic essay written by a current Brown University undergraduate (regardless of concentration) on any topic relating to Jews and Judaism. &nbsp;Papers that use interdisciplinary approaches are especially encouraged. &nbsp;Submissions can include (but are not limited to) a paper used in a course (revised or not), original research, or a thesis chapter that can stand on its own. &nbsp;Submissions should be a maximum of 7000 words, including all notes and bibliography. &nbsp;Only one submission per student is allowed.&nbsp; Submit PDF documents to&nbsp;</span><a href="mailto:judaic@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">judaic@brown.edu</a>&nbsp;w<span>ith subject line "2018 undergraduate essay prize" and&nbsp;</span>your name on each numbered page<span>.&nbsp; Questions?&nbsp; Email&nbsp;</span><a href="mailto:judaic@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">judaic@brown.edu</a>.</p>
<p>DEADLINE&nbsp; FOR SUBMISSION: Monday, March 19, 2018</p>
	</div>

	

	<fieldset class="fieldgroup group-related-files"><legend>Related Files</legend>
		<div class="field field-type-filefield field-field-related-files">
			<div class="field-items">
<div class="field-item odd"><div class="filefield-file"><img class="filefield-icon field-icon-application-pdf"  alt="application/pdf icon" src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/judaic-studies/sites/all/modules/filefield/icons/application-pdf.png" /><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/uploads/2018_Michaelson%20essay%20prize_flyer.pdf" type="application/pdf; length=8220225" title="2018_Michaelson essay prize_flyer.pdf">Poster_2018 Judaic Studies Essay Prize</a></div></div>
			</div>
		</div>
	</fieldset>




</article>
 </div>
                     </div>
        <!-- end .second-column -->

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Program News: Judaic Studies announces 2017-18 Book Group selections</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 

<article class="content news_story clear-block main-article">

<div class="p details">
    <span class="date">June 27, 2017</span>
</div>






<div style="width: 250px; " class="inline-right photo-wrapper main-image">
	  <a href="/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/images/news/2011/books2_0.jpg" class="thickbox"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/imagecache/ursa_feature_image/images/news/2011/books2_0.jpg" alt="" title="" width="250" height="334" class="imagecache imagecache-ursa_feature_image"/></a>
  		  </div>





	<div class="body">
		<h2><span>A message from Professor Michael Satlow about Judaic Studies Book Discussion Group:</span></h2>
<div>
<div>
<div>
<div>I am happy to announce the 2017-18 schedule for the Judaic Studies Book Discussion Group.&nbsp; The purpose of the group is create an opportunity for informal discussion around a book that has been important in the field.&nbsp; All are welcome to attend.&nbsp; Reminders will be sent before each meeting and one copy of each book will be available for loan in the Judaic Studies office.<br /><br />The schedule is below.&nbsp; Each meeting will last take place at noon and last about an hour.&nbsp; Lunch will be provided.<br /><br /></div>
<p><strong>Wednesday, September 13, 2017: David Grossman,&nbsp;<em>A Horse Walks Into a Bar<br /></em></strong>In a little dive in a small Israeli city, Dov Greenstein, a comedian a bit past his prime, is doing a night of stand-up. In the audience is a district court justice, Avishai Lazar, whom Dov knew as a boy, along with a few others who remember Dov as an awkward, scrawny kid who walked on his hands to confound the neighborhood bullies. Gradually, as it teeters between hilarity and hysteria, Dov’s patter becomes a kind of memoir, taking us back into the terrors of his childhood: we meet his beautiful flower of a mother, a Holocaust survivor in need of constant monitoring, and his punishing father, a striver who had little understanding of his creative son. Finally, recalling his week at a military camp for youth—where Lazar witnessed what would become the central event of Dov’s childhood—Dov describes the indescribable while Lazar wrestles with his own part in the comedian’s story of loss and survival. Continuing his investigations into how people confront life’s capricious battering, and how art may blossom from it, Grossman delivers a stunning performance in this memorable one-night engagement (jokes in questionable taste included).<br /><br /></p></div>
<p><strong>Wednesday, November 8, 2017:&nbsp; Aaron Hughes,&nbsp;</strong><em><strong>Jacob Neusner: An American Jewish Iconoclast</strong><br /></em>Jacob Neusner (born 1932) is one of the most important figures in the shaping of modern American Judaism. He was pivotal in transforming the study of Judaism from an insular project only conducted by—and of interest to—religious adherents to one which now flourishes in the secular setting of the university. He is also one of the most colorful, creative, and difficult figures in the American academy. But even those who disagree with Neusner’s academic approach to ancient rabbinic texts have to engage with his pioneering methods.&nbsp;&nbsp;<br />In this comprehensive biography, Aaron Hughes shows Neusner to be much more than a scholar of rabbinics.&nbsp; He is a social commentator, a post-Holocaust theologian, and was an outspoken political figure during the height of the cultural wars of the 1980s. Neusner’s life reflects the story of what happened as Jews migrated to the suburbs in the late 1940s, daring to imagine new lives for themselves as they successfully integrated into the fabric of American society. It is also the story of how American Jews tried to make sense of the world in the aftermath of the extermination of European Jewry and the subsequent creation of the State of Israel in 1948, and how they sought to define what it meant to be an American Jew. &nbsp;<br /><br />Unlike other great American Jewish thinkers, Neusner was born in the U.S., and his Judaism was informed by an American ethos. His Judaism is open, informed by and informing the world. It is an American Judaism, one that has enabled American Jews—the freest in history—to be fully American and fully Jewish.<br /><br /></p></div>
<p><strong>Wednesday, February 7, 2018:&nbsp; Benjamin Sommer,&nbsp;<em>Revelation and Authority</em>:&nbsp;<em>Sinai in Jewish Scripture and Tradition</em></strong><br />At once a study of biblical theology and modern Jewish thought, this volume describes a “participatory theory of revelation” as it addresses the ways biblical authors and contemporary theologians alike understand the process of revelation and hence the authority of the law. Benjamin Sommer maintains that the Pentateuch’s authors intend not only to convey God’s will but to express Israel’s interpretation of and response to that divine will. Thus Sommer’s close readings of biblical texts bolster liberal theologies of modern Judaism, especially those of Abraham Joshua Heschel and Franz Rosenzweig. This bold view of revelation puts a premium on human agency and attests to the grandeur of a God who accomplishes a providential task through the free will of the human subjects under divine authority. Yet, even though the Pentateuch’s authors hold diverse views of revelation, all of them regard the binding authority of the law as sacrosanct. Sommer’s book demonstrates why a law-observant religious Jew can be open to discoveries about the Bible that seem nontraditional or even antireligious.<br /><br /></p></div>
<p><strong>Wednesday, April 18, 2018:&nbsp; Cynthia Baker,&nbsp;</strong><em><strong>Jew</strong><br /></em><em>Jew</em><span>.&nbsp; The word possesses an uncanny power to provoke and unsettle. For millennia,&nbsp;</span><em>Jew</em><span>&nbsp;has signified the consummate&nbsp;</span><em>Other</em><span>, a persistent fly in the ointment of Western civilization’s grand narratives and cultural projects. Only very recently, however, has&nbsp;</span><em>Jew&nbsp;</em><span>been reclaimed as a term of self-identification and pride. &nbsp;</span><span>&nbsp;</span></p>
<div>With these insights as a point of departure, this book offers a wide-ranging exploration of the key word&nbsp;<em>Jew</em>—a term that lies not only at the heart of Jewish experience, but indeed at the core of Western civilization. Examining scholarly debates about the origins and early meanings of&nbsp;<em>Jew</em>, Cynthia M. Baker interrogates categories like “ethnicity,” “race,” and “religion” that inevitably feature in attempts to define the word. Tracing the term’s evolution, she also illuminates its many contradictions, revealing how&nbsp;<em>Jew</em>&nbsp;has served as a marker of materialism and intellectualism, socialism and capitalism, worldly cosmopolitanism and clannish parochialism, chosen status, and accursed stigma.</div>
<div>&nbsp;</div>
<div>Baker proceeds to explore the complex challenges that attend the modern appropriation of Jew as a term of self-identification, with forays into Yiddish language and culture, as well as meditations on&nbsp;<em>Jew</em>-as-identity by contemporary public intellectuals. Finally, by tracing the phrase&nbsp;<em>new Jews</em>&nbsp;through a range of contexts—including the early Zionist movement, current debates about Muslim immigration to Europe, and recent sociological studies in the United States—the book provides a glimpse of what the word&nbsp;<em>Jew</em>&nbsp;is coming to mean in an era of Internet cultures, genetic sequencing, precarious nationalisms, and proliferating identities.</div>
<p><span style="color:#888888">--&nbsp;<br /> </span></p>
<div class="m_-6646093501564386467m_-4948064081086641591gmail_signature">
<div>Michael L. Satlow<br />Professor of Religious Studies and Judaic Studies<br />Box 1826<br />Brown University<br />Providence, RI 02912<br /><a href="tel:(401)%20863-3911" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">(401) 863-3911</a><br /><a href="http://www.mlsatlow.com/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">http://www.mlsatlow.com</a></div>
</div>
	</div>

  <!-- Layout -->
  
  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Program News: Judaic Studies welcomes Visiting Professor Lawrence Wills, Th.D.</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 

<article class="content news_story clear-block main-article">

<div class="p details">
    <span class="date">July 6, 2017</span>
</div>






<div style="width: 244px; " class="inline-right photo-wrapper main-image">
	  <a href="/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/images/news/2011/Lawrene Wills headshot.jpg" class="thickbox"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/judaic-studies/sites/brown.edu.academics.judaic-studies/files/imagecache/ursa_feature_image/images/news/2011/Lawrene%20Wills%20headshot.jpg" alt="" title="" width="244" height="367" class="imagecache imagecache-ursa_feature_image"/></a>
  		  </div>





	<div class="body">
		<p>Judaic Studies is pleased to announce the appointment of Lawrence Wills as Visiting Professor of Religious Studies and Judaic Studies. Professor Wills (Th.D., Harvard Divinity School) taught at Harvard Divinity School, Episcopal Divinity School, and at Wesleyan University before coming to Brown.&nbsp;<span>Among his books are&nbsp;</span><em>The Jewish Novel in the Ancient World&nbsp;</em><span>and&nbsp;</span><em>Not God’s People: Insiders and Outsiders in the Biblical World</em><span>. He is co-editor of the&nbsp;</span><em>Jewish Annotated Apocrypha&nbsp;</em><span>(in progress) and is on the editorial board of&nbsp;</span><em>Journal of Biblical Literature</em><span>.</span></p>
	</div>

	




</article>
 </div>
                     </div>
        <!-- end .second-column -->

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Undergraduate</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-12" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h2><span style="color:#2f2a20">Undergraduate Concentration</span></h2>
<p>Jews have lived and flourished over thousands of years in a variety of social contexts, stretching from the Land of Israel and the eastern Mediterranean to Asia, Africa, Europe, and the Americas. Judaic studies concentrators will have the opportunity to study Jews in these contexts, getting to know their social structures, and what they have created. The subjects of study cover an astonishing range, including Jewish history and society, Jewish law and philosophy, Jewish literature and ritual. Students will learn to unlock this wealth in both the ancient and the modern worlds through a number of academic disciplines - History, Religious Studies, and Literature. These also provide tools for studying and analyzing human societies and cultures in general, for which Jewish experiences provide an important perspective.</p>
<p><strong><a href="https://vivo.brown.edu/display/rrojansk">Rachel Rojanski</a></strong><br />Associate Professor of Judaic Studies and History<br />Undergraduate Concentration Advisor<br /><a href="mailto:Rachel_Rojanski@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">Rachel_Rojanski@brown.edu</a></p>
<p><strong><a href="http://research.brown.edu/myresearch/Ruth_Adler_Ben-Yehuda">Ruth Adler Ben-Yehuda</a></strong><br />Senior Lecturer in Judaic Studies&nbsp;<br />Undergraduate Concentration Advisor<br />&nbsp;<a href="mailto:Ruth_Adler_Ben_Yehuda@Brown.edu" rel="nofollow">Ruth_Adler_Ben_Yehuda@brown.edu</a></p>
  </div>

                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Concentration Requirements</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-221" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p><strong>Required Coursework for concentrators declaring in Spring 2013 and beyond</strong></p>
<p>The Program in Judaic Studies offers two paths (detailed below). Please note that the following apply to each concentrator:</p>
<p>1) All students are required to take a total of <strong>ten</strong> courses.</p>
<p>2) All students must take one full year of Hebrew (two of the ten  required courses). Generally, this requirement will consist of two  courses in Elementary Hebrew (JS 0100/0200) or the equivalent as  determined by a proficiency examination. Fulfillment of the Hebrew  requirement through examination does not reduce the requirement to take  ten courses for the concentration.</p>
<p class="number">3) Upon declaring a concentration in Judaic studies,  each student must define his or her primary disciplinary track (History,  Religious Studies, or Language/Literature). Concentrators will then be  assigned a faculty mentor in that discipline (within the JS faculty) to  help the students select courses and construct a coherent concentration  plan.&nbsp;</p>
<p style="padding-left:30px"><strong>Program in History or Religious Studies:&nbsp; </strong></p>
<p class="number" style="padding-left:30px">For  this track, students are expected to complete a minimum of four courses  in their area of disciplinary focus (History or Religious Studies), at  least one of which must and no more than two of which may be outside the  program in Judaic Studies in the department of disciplinary focus  (preferably methods courses, such as in the History department or RELS  1000). Students in this track, in consultation with the concentration  adviser and faculty mentor, may apply up to two additional Hebrew  language courses (JUDS 0300, JUDS 0400, or JUDS 0500) to the additional  four required courses for the concentration.</p>
<p class="number" style="padding-left:30px"><strong>Program in Language/Literature:</strong></p>
<p class="number" style="padding-left:30px">For  this track, students are expected to complete five courses in Hebrew  language (JUDS 0100/JUDS 0200; JUDS 0300/JUDS 0400; JUDS 0500). In  addition, students will take Issues in Israel in Hebrew (JUDS 0600) and  one further course in Judaic Studies (within the disciplinary focus).  Two additional courses in the disciplinary focus, at least one of which  must be outside the Program in Judaic Studies in a department of shared  disciplinary focus (e.g. English or Comparative Literature), are also  required. Fulfillment of the Hebrew requirement through proficiency  examination does not reduce the requirement to take ten courses for the  concentration.&nbsp;</p>
<p class="number">4) Of the courses required in the Program in Judaic  Studies, at least one should focus on the ancient period and one should  focus on the modern period.</p>
<p class="number">5) Each student, in discussion with his/her mentor, is  required to designate an advanced course (1000 level) in his/her senior  year either within the Judaic Studies program or in the corresponding  disciplinary department as the capstone for his/her concentration.  Within the frame of this capstone course, the concentrator will write a  final paper on a topic in Judaic Studies that displays the in an  appropriate way the theoretical and interpretive issues of the  concentration focus. If students opt to fulfill this requirement in a  course outside the Program in Judaic Studies, the student must get  permission in advance both from his/her mentor and from the professor of  the course in question since the student’s final project will address a  Judaic studies topic or theme.</p>
<p class="number">6) Students who study at other institutions, either in  the United States or abroad, may apply a maximum of four courses (two  topical and two language courses) to the concentration.</p>
<p class="number">7) Double concentrators may count up to two courses  that they have used to complete their concentration requirements in  another department towards their concentraion in Judaic Studies.</p>
  </div>

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Department Undergraduate Group</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-220" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h4>Judaic Studies Department Undergraduate Group</h4>
<p>The Judaic Studies DUG is an organization for students concentrating  in or interested in the Judaic Studies Program. The DUG meets several  times each semester and is meant to bring together students within the  department through organized meetings and events. The DUG is meant to be  a resource for students to share information, discuss their interest in  Judaic Studies, and interact with faculty members.<br /><br />For more  information about the DUG, including meeting times and upcoming events,  please contact the Judaic Studies Academic Program Manager, Susan Rottenberg at <a href="mailto:susan_rottenberg@brown.edu">susan_rottenberg@brown.edu</a>.</p>
<h4><span>Join the Judaic Studies Listerv</span></h4>
<p>The Brown Judaic Studies listserv exists as a means to share events and notices, and to provide a venue to engage in dialogue with fellow undergraduate concentrators, students in Judaic Studies classes, and others interested in this field of study.</p>
  </div>

                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Honors Thesis</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-222" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p align="center"><strong>Writing an Honors Thesis in Judaic Studies</strong><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><span>For thousands of years, Jews, their religion, their history, and their culture have been the subject of significant scholarly attention.&nbsp; We encourage you to make your own contribution to this tradition by doing original research on some aspect of Judaic Studies and writing an honors thesis.</span></p>
<p><span>To do this, you will need to be a concentrator in JS with an outstanding record (A- average) in JS courses and you will be expected to write a full academic paper of some 35,000-55,000 words based on your own original work.&nbsp;&nbsp; As in all research, you will need to invest your time in planning, reading, thinking, and writing.&nbsp;&nbsp; Before you embark on the project, discuss it with the Concentration Adviser, to make sure that it is the right choice for you.&nbsp; Do this in your sixth semester.&nbsp;&nbsp; With the help of the C.A. identify a faculty member who will work with you as you write your thesis.&nbsp; Like everything else, research skills need to be learned, and your thesis director is there to help you learn them.</span></p>
<p><span>Together with your director, you will think about a topic that really interests you and begin to hone it into a research project.&nbsp; You will also choose a second reader for your paper, who, though less involved than your director, will help you by bringing you different perspectives – and different research skills.&nbsp; By the end of the sixth semester, you should be set, with a topic, list of primary sources, basic bibliography, and plan of research and writing.&nbsp; You will submit these for approval in a formal thesis proposal early in the seventh semester.</span></p>
<p><span>Then it’s up to you!&nbsp; Independent reading and research are what it is all about.&nbsp; You will meet with your director on a regular basis to make sure that you are on track and to help you learn the tricks of our trade (what we call, critical research methodology and a logical research strategy).&nbsp; Once you are ready to write, your director will help you plan your thesis and work with you on each chapter in turn.&nbsp; All told, research and writing will take you two semesters.</span></p>
<p><span>At the end of the process, you will have written a genuine piece of scholarship.&nbsp;&nbsp; Based on original research and the close reading of primary sources, your thesis will present an argument based on your own analysis and will engage an ongoing debate or discussion in the field you have chosen.&nbsp; You will be able to demonstrate your knowledge of the major research done in that field until now and identify your own contribution. &nbsp;Clearly and precisely written, the final thesis should also have a complete scientific apparatus – citations and a full bibliography – and so can take its place in the research library of the field.</span></p>
<p><span>Once it has been accepted and graded by your director and the second reader, your thesis will be discussed by the faculty members of the Judaic Studies Program as they vote on granting you Honors.&nbsp; It will then be published on the Program’s website and made available to a wide audience of scholars and interested readers. &nbsp;&nbsp;On Degree Day, you will be a member of a select group of students awarded BA with Honors.&nbsp; Congratulations!</span></p>
<p align="center"><strong>Timetable</strong></p>
<ol>
<li><span style="text-decoration:underline">Sixth Semester: Planning and Organization</span></li>
<li>Speak to J.S. Concentration Adviser about writing an honors thesis.</li>
<li>Find a thesis director and, in consultation, choose a topic and plan the contours of the project.</li>
<li>Choose a second reader.</li>
<li>By the end of the semester, submit a thesis information form, with brief details of your project, countersigned by director and second reader.</li>
</ol>
<ol>
<li><span style="text-decoration:underline">Seventh Semester: Research and Reading</span></li>
<li>Enroll in JUDS 1975.</li>
<li>Write a thesis proposal (1,500-2,000) words to be submitted by the end of week three.</li>
<li>Formally enroll in Honors Program by the end of week four.</li>
<li>Research and reading – regular meetings with director.</li>
</ol>
<ol>
<li><span style="text-decoration:underline">Eighth Semester: Research and Writing</span></li>
<li>Enroll in JUDS 1976</li>
<li>Complete research and reading early in the semester.</li>
<li>Consult with adviser about writing plan.</li>
<li>Write the director, holding regular meetings with adviser (and also second reader) to discuss drafts.</li>
<li>Submit thesis no later than April 15 for May graduates and November 15 for December completers.</li>
</ol>
<p><span>For further details, consult the detailed description of the JS Honors Program in the section on the Judaic Studies concentration in the University Bulletin:&nbsp;&nbsp; http://bulletin.brown.edu/the-college/concentrations/juds/</span></p>
  </div>
